    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A chromosome-scale reference genome of Aquilegia oxysepala var. kansuensis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-07-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The genus Aquilegia (Ranunculaceae) has been cultivated as ornamental and medicinal plants for centuries. With petal spurs of strikingly diverse size and shape, Aquilegia has also been recognized as an excellent system for evolutionary studies. Pollinator&#8208;mediated selection for longer spurs is believed to have shaped the evolution of this genus, especially the North American taxa. Recently, however, an opposite evolutionary trend was reported in an Asian lineage, where multiple origins of mini- or even nonspurred morphs have occurred. Interesting as it is, the lack of genomic resources has limited our ability to decipher the molecular and evolutionary mechanisms underlying spur reduction in this special lineage. Using long-read sequencing (PacBio Sequel), in combination with optical maps (BioNano DLS) and Hi&#8211;C, we assembled a high-quality reference genome of A. oxysepala var. kansuensis, a sister species to the nonspurred taxon. The final assembly is approximately 293.2&#8201;Mb, 94.6% (277.4&#8201;Mb) of which has been anchored to 7 pseudochromosomes. A total of 25,571 protein-coding genes were predicted, with 97.2% being functionally annotated. When comparing this genome with that of A. coerulea, we detected a large rearrangement between Chr1 and Chr4, which might have caused the Chr4 of A. oxysepala var. kansuensis to partly deviate from the &#8220;decaying&#8221; path that was taken before the split of Aquilegia and Semiaquilegia. This high-quality reference genome is fundamental to further investigations on the development and evolution of petal spurs and provides a strong foundation for the breeding of new horticultural Aquilegia cultivars."/>

    <meta name="description" content="The genus Aquilegia (Ranunculaceae) has been cultivated as ornamental and medicinal plants for centuries. With petal spurs of strikingly diverse size and shape, Aquilegia has also been recognized as an excellent system for evolutionary studies. Pollinator&#8208;mediated selection for longer spurs is believed to have shaped the evolution of this genus, especially the North American taxa. Recently, however, an opposite evolutionary trend was reported in an Asian lineage, where multiple origins of mini- or even nonspurred morphs have occurred. Interesting as it is, the lack of genomic resources has limited our ability to decipher the molecular and evolutionary mechanisms underlying spur reduction in this special lineage. Using long-read sequencing (PacBio Sequel), in combination with optical maps (BioNano DLS) and Hi&#8211;C, we assembled a high-quality reference genome of A. oxysepala var. kansuensis, a sister species to the nonspurred taxon. The final assembly is approximately 293.2&#8201;Mb, 94.6% (277.4&#8201;Mb) of which has been anchored to 7 pseudochromosomes. A total of 25,571 protein-coding genes were predicted, with 97.2% being functionally annotated. When comparing this genome with that of A. coerulea, we detected a large rearrangement between Chr1 and Chr4, which might have caused the Chr4 of A. oxysepala var. kansuensis to partly deviate from the &#8220;decaying&#8221; path that was taken before the split of Aquilegia and Semiaquilegia. This high-quality reference genome is fundamental to further investigations on the development and evolution of petal spurs and provides a strong foundation for the breeding of new horticultural Aquilegia cultivars."/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The genus <i>Aquilegia</i> (Ranunculaceae) has been cultivated as ornamental and medicinal plants for centuries. With petal spurs of strikingly diverse size and shape, <i>Aquilegia</i> has also been recognized as an excellent system for evolutionary studies. Pollinator‐mediated selection for longer spurs is believed to have shaped the evolution of this genus, especially the North American taxa. Recently, however, an opposite evolutionary trend was reported in an Asian lineage, where multiple origins of mini- or even nonspurred morphs have occurred. Interesting as it is, the lack of genomic resources has limited our ability to decipher the molecular and evolutionary mechanisms underlying spur reduction in this special lineage. Using long-read sequencing (PacBio Sequel), in combination with optical maps (BioNano DLS) and Hi–C, we assembled a high-quality reference genome of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>, a sister species to the nonspurred taxon. The final assembly is approximately 293.2 Mb, 94.6% (277.4 Mb) of which has been anchored to 7 pseudochromosomes. A total of 25,571 protein-coding genes were predicted, with 97.2% being functionally annotated. When comparing this genome with that of <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i>, we detected a large rearrangement between Chr1 and Chr4, which might have caused the Chr4 of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> to partly deviate from the “decaying” path that was taken before the split of <i>Aquilegia</i> and <i>Semiaquilegia</i>. This high-quality reference genome is fundamental to further investigations on the development and evolution of petal spurs and provides a strong foundation for the breeding of new horticultural <i>Aquilegia</i> cultivars.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Aquilegia</i> (Ranunculaceae), commonly known as columbine, consists of ~70 species that are widely distributed in the temperate zones of the Northern Hemisphere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Whittall, J. B., Medina-Marino, A., Zimmer, E. A. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Generating single-copy nuclear gene data for a recent adaptive radiation. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol.39, 124–134 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d27764e561">1</a></sup>. Numerous species and varieties of this genus have been cultivated as garden ornamentals for centuries due to their attractive flowers with unusual characteristics, including petaloid sepals and petal spurs of diverse shape and size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kramer, E. M. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Aquilegia as a model system for the evolution and ecology of petals. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B365, 477–490 (2010)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d27764e565">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kramer, E. M. Aquilegia: a new model for plant development, ecology, and evolution. Annu. Rev. Plant. Biol.60, 261–277 (2009)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d27764e565_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Hodges, S. A. &amp; Derieg, N. J. Adaptive radiations: from field to genomic studies. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA106, 9947–9954 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d27764e568">4</a></sup>. Particularly, the length of spurs (<span class="stix">∼</span>1–16 cm) varies dramatically, matching the tongue length of the corresponding pollinators; this has made the genus a model system for research on pollinator-driven diversification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Kramer, E. M. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Aquilegia as a model system for the evolution and ecology of petals. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B365, 477–490 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d27764e572">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Whittall, J. B. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Pollinator shifts drive increasingly long nectar spurs in columbine flowers. Nature447, 706–709 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27764e575">5</a></sup>. In fact, the multiple origins of species with longer petal spurs from those with shorter ones in the North American <i>Aquilegia</i> clade have become textbook examples of pollinator shift-mediated adaptive evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Whittall, J. B. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Pollinator shifts drive increasingly long nectar spurs in columbine flowers. Nature447, 706–709 (2007)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27764e583">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hodges, S. A., Whittall, J. B., Fulton, M. &amp; Yang, J. Y. Genetics of floral traits influencing reproductive isolation between Aquilegia formosa and Aquilegia pubescens. Am. Nat.159, S51–S60 (2002)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d27764e583_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fulton, M. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Floral isolation between Aquilegia formosa and Aquilegia pubescens. Philos. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B266, 2247–2252 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d27764e586">7</a></sup>. Recently, however, one Asian <i>Aquilegia</i> lineage was revealed to have experienced multiple origins of mini- or even nonspurred morphs, indicating an opposite trend of spur evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Huang, L. et al. Genetic diversity and evolutionary history of four closely related Aquilegia species revealed by 10 nuclear gene fragments. J. Syst. Evol.56, 129–138 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d27764e593">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Xue, C. et al. Morphological variation pattern of Aquilegia ecalcarata and its relatives. J. Syst. Evol.58, 221–233 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d27764e596">9</a></sup>. The genus, therefore, is also an excellent system for the study of spur reduction. Deciphering the developmental and evolutionary mechanisms underlying these morphological changes would not only facilitate our understanding of species diversification but also provide a good foundation for the breeding of promising horticultural <i>Aquilegia</i> cultivars.</p><p>Genomic resources are essential and attainable for developmental and evolutionary studies. To date, only one genome in the <i>Aquilegia</i> genus has been sequenced (<i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> reference genome v3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e615">10</a></sup>. <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> has flowers with long petal spurs and belongs to the North American clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e626">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Fior, S. et al. Spatiotemporal reconstruction of the Aquilegia rapid radiation through next-generation sequencing of rapidly evolving cpDNA regions. N. Phytol.198, 579–592 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27764e629">11</a></sup>; its genomic resources have facilitated numerous studies concerning spur elongation, adaptation, and speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhu, R. R., Gao, Y., Zhang, Q. X. &amp; Debener, T. Quantitative trait locus mapping of floral and related traits using an F2 population of Aquilegia. Plant Breed.133, 153–161 (2014)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27764e633">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yant, L., Collani, S., Puzey, J., Levy, C. &amp; Kramer, E. M. Molecular basis for three-dimensional elaboration of the Aquilegia petal spur. Proc. R. Soc. Lond. B282, 20142778 (2015)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d27764e633_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Whittall, J. B., Voelckel, C., Kliebenstein, D. J. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Convergence, constraint and the role of gene expression during adaptive radiation: floral anthocyanins in Aquilegia. Mol. Ecol.15, 4645–4657 (2006)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d27764e633_2">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Voelckel, C., Borevitz, J. O., Kramer, E. M. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Within and between whorls: comparative transcriptional profiling of Aquilegia and Arabidopsis. PLoS ONE5, e9735 (2010)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d27764e633_3">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sharma, B., Yant, L., Hodges, S. A. &amp; Kramer, E. M. Understanding the development and evolution of novel floral form in. Aquilegia. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol.17, 22–27 (2014)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d27764e633_4">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Puzey, J. R., Gerbode, S. J., Hodges, S. A., Kramer, E. M. &amp; Mahadevan, L. Evolution of spur-length diversity in Aquilegia petals is achieved solely through cell-shape anisotropy. Proc. R. Soc. Lond. B279, 1640–1645 (2012)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27764e633_5">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ballerini, E. S., Kramer, E. M. &amp; Hodges, S. A. Comparative transcriptomics of early petal development across four diverse species of Aquilegia reveal few genes consistently associated with nectar spur development. BMC Genomics20, 668 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27764e636">18</a></sup>. However, the application of these resources to address the problems of spur reduction is limited because <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> diverged from the Asian lineage approximately 4.8 million years ago (Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Fior, S. et al. Spatiotemporal reconstruction of the Aquilegia rapid radiation through next-generation sequencing of rapidly evolving cpDNA regions. N. Phytol.198, 579–592 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27764e646">11</a></sup>. Moreover, several lines of evidence suggest a “decaying” nature of <i>Aquilegia</i> Chr4, which has likely been evolving under reduced purifying and/or background selection and has a strikingly higher level of polymorphism than the rest of the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e654">10</a></sup>. This further aggravates the difficulty in referring to the genome of a phylogenetically far-related species.</p><p>Here, we report the genome of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig1">1</a>), a species that is sister to the nonspurred taxon in Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Huang, L. et al. Genetic diversity and evolutionary history of four closely related Aquilegia species revealed by 10 nuclear gene fragments. J. Syst. Evol.56, 129–138 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d27764e670">8</a></sup>. We assembled a chromosome-scale reference genome by long-read sequencing (PacBio Sequel system), optical map (BioNano DLS) scaffolding, and further anchoring the scaffolds to pseudochromosomes using Hi–C. Comparison of the <i>A. coerulea</i> and <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> genomes revealed a large chromosomal rearrangement between Chr1 and Chr4, and we cataloged the species-specific genes. Notably, we found that, unlike Chr4 in other <i>Aquilegia</i> taxa, this chromosome in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> is not evolving entirely under reduced purifying selection due to the rearrangement event.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="256"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Images of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome size and heterozygosity estimation</h3><p>We used a single individual of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> that was collected from Yuzhong County, Gansu Province, China, for whole-genome sequencing. We noticed that a new name, <i>A</i>. <i>yangii</i>, has recently been given to plants of this and many other populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, L., Luo, Y., Yang, C., Deng, J. &amp; Erst, A. S. Aquilegia yangii (Ranunculaceae), a new species from western China. Phytotaxa348, 289–296 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d27764e730">19</a></sup>. However, we decided to stay with the original name because the new one is still poorly known. To guide genome sequencing and assembly, we estimated the genome size of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> using flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Dolezel, J. &amp; Bartos, J. Plant DNA flow cytometry and estimation of nuclear genome size. Ann. Bot.95, 99–110 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d27764e741">20</a></sup> and K-mer analysis. Briefly, flow cytometry indicated that <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> had a genome size of 312 Mb (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S1</a>). For K-mer analysis, we obtained 18.3 Gb short paired-end reads by Illumina sequencing; 18.2 Gb were retained after the removal of low-quality reads (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S1</a>). Based on the total number of 17-mers and the depth of the main peak in the 17-mer frequency distribution (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S2</a>), we estimated that the genome size and heterozygosity rate of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> were 349 Mb and 0.15%, respectively (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S2</a>). To ensure we had enough data for genome assembly, we decided to use 349 Mb as a reference for further sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Sequencing and assembly of the genome</h3><p>To obtain a high-quality genome, three technologies were applied: PacBio SMRT sequencing (36.7 Gb, ~105×; Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S3</a>, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig2">2a</a>), BioNano DLS optical mapping (102.1 Gb, ~293×; Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S4</a>, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig2">2a</a>) and Hi-C mapping (31.9 Gb, ~91×; Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S5</a>, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig2">2a</a>). The PacBio long-read assemblies showed high contiguity and resulted in a total of only 852 contigs with an N50 of 2.2 Mb, and the longest one was approximately 7.8 Mb (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S6</a>). After adding the optical mapping data from BioNano DLS, we were able to assemble the contigs into scaffolds. A total of 663 scaffolds (total length = 297.8 Mb) were produced, among which 21 were hybrid scaffolds (total length = 284.8 Mb) encompassing, in most cases, entire chromosome arms. The longest scaffold reached 41.4 Mb, and the N50 was 40.9 Mb. We then anchored all these scaffolds to 7 pseudochromosomes using the Hi–C data in 3D de novo assembly (3D-DNA) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d27764e800">21</a></sup>. Eventually, a high-quality chromosome-level <i>A. oxysepala</i> var<i>. kansuensis</i> assembly (679 scaffolds) was obtained, with 7 pseudochromosomes accounting for 94.6% (277.4/293.2) of the total genome length (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome assembly of A. oxysepala var. kansuensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Genome assembly of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="544"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Summary of sequencing data used for genome assembly. Sequencing coverage was calculated based on the genome size obtained from K-mer analysis (349 Mb). For BioNano, effective coverage of the reference is shown. <b>b</b> Hi–C heatmap showing interactions among the 7 pseudochromosomes. <b>c</b> Alignment between the BioNano DLS maps and the final genome assembly</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation statistics of the <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Five approaches were utilized to evaluate the quality of the assembly. First, the Illumina short reads were mapped back to the assembled contigs using Burrows–Wheeler Aligner (BWA) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d27764e958">22</a></sup>. The mapping rate of paired-end reads reached 97.2%, indicating high completeness and accuracy of the final assembly. Second, to assess the completeness of the assembly, we performed benchmarking universal single-copy ortholog (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d27764e962">23</a></sup> analysis by searching against the 1440 conserved single-copy genes in plants and identified 1342 (93.2%) complete BUSCOs (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Tab1">1</a>). Third, when the BioNano assembly consensus genome maps (CMAPs) were aligned to the 7 in silico maps of the <i>A. oxysepala</i> var<i>. kansuensis</i>, a total of 277.3 Mb (unique aligned length) were covered, validating 94.6% of the assembly (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S7</a>). Fourth, we extracted single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) of the whole-genome using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d27764e979">24</a></sup> and found that the proportions of heterozygous and homozygous SNPs were 0.022% and 0.001%, respectively, suggesting high accuracy of the assembly. Finally, we used the LTR assembly index (LAI)—a standard for evaluating the assembly of repeat sequences—to assess assembly continuity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res.46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d27764e983">25</a></sup>. We found that the LAI score of the <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> assembly reached 16.7, which was much higher than that of <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> (LAI = 12.6). Both genomes could be classified as reference quality, similar to the quality of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> TAIR10 genome (LAI = 14.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res.46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1006">25</a></sup>. Taken together, these results suggest that the genome assembly had very high continuity, completeness, and correctness.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Annotation of the genome</h3><p>Two methods (i.e., homology alignment and de novo annotation) were used to identify repeats in the assembly. Among the five major types of repeats detected (DNA transposon, LINE, LTR, SINE, and unknown), LTRs comprised the largest proportion (35.88%, total length 105.2 Mb). Unknown repeats ranked second, occupying 6.46% (total length 18.9 Mb) of the genome. DNA transposons, LINEs, and SINEs accounted for 2.27%, 1.06%, and 0.01% of the genome, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig3">3a, d</a>). Thus, altogether, 45.68% of the genome was predicted to be repeats.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Annotation of the A. oxysepala var. kansuensis genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Annotation of the <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="620"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of genes and different types of repeat elements: <b>I</b>, Legends of seven pseudochromosomes; <b>II</b>, gene density; <b>III</b>, transposon density; <b>IV</b>, transposable element protein density; and <b>V</b>, tandem repeat density. <b>b</b> Number of genes predicted by different approaches: ab initio (yellow), homology-based (pink) and transcriptome-assisted (gray). <b>c</b> Number of functionally annotated genes based on various databases. <b>d</b> Accumulation history of different kinds of repeat elements</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A combination of three methods, including homology-based prediction, ab initio prediction and transcriptome-assisted prediction, was employed to identify protein-coding genes. Sequences from six species were used as references (see Section “Materials and methods”) for homology-based prediction, which generated a total of 22,282 gene models (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig3">3b</a>). For ab initio prediction, a list of 24,853 putative gene models (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig3">3b</a>) was obtained by integrating the results from several software packages (see Section “Materials and methods”). To enhance transcriptome-assisted prediction, we performed Iso-Seq and RNA-Seq on various plant tissue samples of different developmental stages (see Section “Materials and methods”). Specifically, Iso-Seq was conducted on five different samples, yielding 46.7 Gb clean data; pooled (equal-amount) total RNA from these samples was also subjected to RNA-Seq, producing 12.8 Gb clean data (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S8</a>). By applying these data, 18,986 genes were identified (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig3">3b</a>). The three datasets were then merged together to generate a nonredundant reference gene set containing 25,571 protein-coding genes, with 24,913 on the 7 chromosomes and 658 on the unassigned scaffolds (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig3">3b</a>). These genes had transcripts that were on average 3,447 bp, including 5 exons with a mean length of 242 bp. We further detected that 24,744, 20,286, 23,692, and 20,374 genes showed significant similarity to known proteins in the NR, Swiss-Prot, InterPro, and KEGG databases, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig3">3c</a>). Integration of the four datasets led to the assignment of potential functions to 24,846 (97.2%) of the 25,571 protein-coding genes in the <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> genome. We also identified 930 tRNAs, 483 rRNAs (5S, 5.8S, 18S, and 28S), 696 snRNAs, and 275 miRNAs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparison of the genomes of the two <i>Aquilegia</i> taxa</h3><p>Using MUMmer software (v3.23), we aligned the <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> genome assembly with the <i>A. coerulea</i> genome (v3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1113">10</a></sup>. For convenience, the chromosomes of these two species will hereafter be referred to as A.ox_chr1–7 and A.co_chr1–7, respectively. The two genomes showed extensive synteny except for one large rearrangement between Chr1 and Chr4 (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig4">4a, b</a>). Specifically, relative to the <i>A. coerulea</i> genome, there was a reciprocal chromosomal translocation between A.ox_chr1 and A.ox_chr4. To confirm the correct assembly around the breakpoints of the translocated regions, we performed two different analyses. First, we mapped all PacBio long reads to our assembly to determine if there were misjoins caused by a shortage of read mapping evidence near the breaking area. The results showed that the mapping coverage spanning and flanking the area was similar to that in other regions (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S3</a>). Second, we rearranged the two concerned chromosomes, referring to the <i>A. coerulea</i> genome, and thereby created two hypothesized chromosomes (h_A.ox_chr1 and h_A.ox_chr4) that shared chromosome-wide synteny with A.co_chr1 and A.co_chr4, respectively. We then mapped the Hi–C data to h_A.ox_chr1 and h_A.ox_chr4 using Juicer software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst.3, 95–98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1130">26</a></sup>. If the two hypothesized chromosomes represented the correct assembly, we would expect a smoother Hi–C interaction heatmap. On the contrary, we observed obvious chromogram discontinuities, indicating misjoins (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S4b</a>). Likewise, when aligning the Hi–C data to the genome of <i>A. coerulea</i>, we also found apparent conflicts on A.co_chr1 and A.co_chr4 (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S4c</a>). These results together suggest that the rearrangement between Chr1 and Chr4 is likely to be real. We further zoomed in the rearrangement region to examine whether there were special genes located around the breakpoints. Adjacent syntenic blocks were compared in detail to determine the exact break sites. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig4">4c</a>, the 32.88–33.33 Mb region on A.co_chr1 could be aligned to the 31.47–31.6 Mb of A.ox_chr1, while the downstream 33.31–36.17 Mb aligned to the 29.10–31.00 Mb region of A.ox_chr4. The 29.37–31.18 Mb region of A.co_chr4 was alignable with the 27.08–28.80 Mb region of A.ox_chr4, whereas the downstream 31.77–33.67 Mb shared synteny with the 32.80–33.96 Mb region of A.ox_chr1. From these results, we determined the breakpoint regions, including the 31.60–32.80 Mb region on A.ox_chr1 and the 28.80–29.10 Mb region on A.ox_chr4. These two fragments each contained 16 and 7 genes (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig4">4c</a>), among which 3 and 2, respectively, had no homologous counterparts in the <i>A. coerulea</i> genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genome comparison between A. oxysepala var. kansuensis and A. coerulea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Genome comparison between <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="606"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Dot-plot alignment between the two taxa. <b>b</b> Circos plot showing syntenic relationships and chromosomal rearrangements between the two genomes. <b>c</b> Synteny of genes located in and around the breakpoint regions of chromosomes 1 and 4. Red braces stand for the breakpoint regions in <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>. <b>d</b> Comparison of gene numbers in different classes of orthologous groups</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Compared with the genome of <i>A. coerulea</i> (v3.1), in which 30,023 protein-coding genes were annotated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1199">10</a></sup>, the genome of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> contained 4452 fewer genes. To further understand the gene content differences between these two genomes, we first clustered their genes into different orthogroups using OrthoFinder software (v2.3.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol.20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1209">27</a></sup>. Among the 55,594 genes, 17,391 pairs were one-to-one orthologs. A total of 14,160 genes (4601 in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and 9559 in <i>A. coerulea</i>) formed clusters (X:0 clusters, X ≥ 1) with genes from only one of the two species (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig4">4d</a>). Although in other gene clusters, copy number differences were found between these two species, the aggregate numbers (3579 vs. 3073) were similar (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig4">4d</a>). Thus, the possession of more X:0 clusters in <i>A. coerulea</i> was the main reason for the interspecies gene number discrepancy. We further conducted a new round of ortholog clustering to identify genes in these X:0 clusters that were likely to be species-specific by including sequences from <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, and <i>Papaver somniferum</i>. This analysis revealed that 2250 and 5119 genes were specific to <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>, respectively.</p><p>Moreover, we classified genes into different families to further elucidate the expansion/contraction differences in certain categories between <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig5">5a</a>). By referring to a database where 13,867 genes in <i>A. coerulea</i> were assigned to 985 families, we were able to put 13,860 genes from <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> into 955 families. We found that the two species showed clear differences in the number of genes in some functionally important families (e.g., DUF, F-box, CBM, cytochrome P450, and MADS-box) (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig5">5b, c</a> and Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S9</a>). For example, this detailed analysis identified 65 and 88 MADS-box genes from <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>, respectively. In line with the contrast evolutionary patterns of Type I (fast birth-and-death) and Type II (highly conserved) MADS-box genes, the two species contained comparable numbers of Type II genes, while <i>A. coerulea</i> contained 18 more Type I genes (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig5">5c</a>). Because Type II genes play important roles in flower development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Smaczniak, C., Immink, R. G. H., Angenent, G. C. &amp; Kaufmann, K. Developmental and evolutionary diversity of plant MADS-domain factors: insights from recent studies. Development139, 3081–3098 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1305">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Theissen, G., Melzer, R. &amp; Rumpler, F. MADS-domain transcription factors and the floral quartet model of flower development: linking plant development and evolution. Development143, 3259–3271 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1308">29</a></sup>, we further examined the copy number difference in each subfamily between the two species. In each of the <i>APETALA1</i> (<i>AP1</i>), <i>SEPALLATA</i> (<i>SEP</i>), <i>APETALA3</i> (<i>AP3</i>), and <i>SHORT VEGETATIVE PHASE</i> (<i>SVP</i>) subfamilies, <i>A. coerulea</i> contained one more copy. Genes in the first three subfamilies usually determine floral organ identity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Smaczniak, C., Immink, R. G. H., Angenent, G. C. &amp; Kaufmann, K. Developmental and evolutionary diversity of plant MADS-domain factors: insights from recent studies. Development139, 3081–3098 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1341">28</a></sup>, while those in the <i>SVP</i> subfamily control flowering time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Smaczniak, C., Immink, R. G. H., Angenent, G. C. &amp; Kaufmann, K. Developmental and evolutionary diversity of plant MADS-domain factors: insights from recent studies. Development139, 3081–3098 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1348">28</a></sup>. These differences may have impacted the difference in flower traits between <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>; further investigations on these genes would be of interest.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Gene family comparison between A.oxysepala var. kansuensis and A. coerulea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Gene family comparison between <i>A.</i><i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="953"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> The number of gene families in each size category and the number of genes from each species. The size of each gene family was defined by referring to the genes in <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i>. Note that gene family classifications are not mutually exclusive. <b>b</b> Comparison of gene numbers in the top 15 families (ranked by size). <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree (neighbor joining, NJ) of MADS-box genes from the two species constructed with the <i>p</i>-distance model (1000 bootstrap replications)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Previous studies have suggested that the entire Chr4 in <i>Aquilegia</i> had a unique evolutionary pattern, with approximately twice the level of polymorphism of the rest of the genome, which might have been caused by reduced purifying selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1406">10</a></sup>. For this reason, we performed two types of analyses. First, we calculated the density of genes on all seven chromosomes (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig6">6a</a>). We found that the density of genes on A.ox_chr4 was significantly lower than that on the other chromosomes (Benjamini–Hochberg adjusted <i>P</i> ≤ 0.01, one-sided Mann–Whitney <i>U</i> test; Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig6">6b</a> and Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S10</a>). We then calculated the <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> ratio of all one-to-one orthologs between <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>. Regardless of the translocation, when comparing genes on A.ox_chr4 with those on the other chromosomes, we found that the <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> ratios on this chromosome were the highest, although not every comparison was significant after multiple-testing correction (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig7">7a</a> and Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S11</a>). This indicates that genes on this chromosome are likely to be evolving under fewer functional constraints.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Distribution and significance test of gene density."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Distribution and significance test of gene density.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="553"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of gene number in each 300 kb window. The arrows on chromosomes 1 and 4 designate rearrangement breakpoints. Pink arrows show that A.ox_chr1_2 is homologous to A.co_chr4_2, while dark-green arrows show the homology of A.ox_chr4_2 with A.co_chr1_2. <b>b</b> Boxplot of gene density on each chromosome. Each point represents the number of genes in a 300 kb window. In the boxplots, each box indicates the lower quartile, median, and upper quartile values. Pairwise significance tests between Chr4 and the other chromosomes within each species were performed. Asterisks indicate significance level: **<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, ***<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001. Detailed <i>P</i> value for each comparison is provided in Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S10</a>. <b>c</b> Gene density on different homologous fragments. Detailed <i>P</i> value for each comparison is provided in Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S12</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Distribution and significance test of dN/dS values."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Distribution and significance test of <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0328-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_328_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="302"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>a</b> Boxplot of <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values calculated between one-to-one orthologs. Genes were assigned to different chromosomes according to the <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> genome. Pairwise significance tests between Chr4 and the other chromosomes were performed. Asterisks indicate significance level: *<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, **<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01, ***<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001. Detailed <i>P</i> value for each comparison is provided in Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S11</a>. <b>b</b> Boxplot of <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values for one-to-one orthologs distributed on the rearranged chromosome segments. Detailed <i>P</i> value for each comparison is provided in Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S13</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>However, because there was a rearrangement between A.ox_chr4 and A.ox_chr1, it was not clear whether the entire A.ox_chr4 or just the part that was homologous to A.co_chr4 had this evolutionary pattern. We thus separated the two focal chromosomes into four parts (i.e., A.ox_chr1_1, A.ox_chr1_2, A.ox_chr4_1, and A.ox_chr4_2) according to their homologous relationships with the <i>A. coerulea</i> genome and made further comparisons. We found that A.ox_chr4_1 and A.ox_chr1_2, which were homologous to Chr4 of <i>A. coerulea</i>, had the lowest gene density (Benjamini–Hochberg adjusted <i>P</i> &lt; 0.001, one-sided Mann–Whitney <i>U</i> test; Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig6">6c</a>, Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S12</a>). When comparing <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values, not surprisingly, we found that the highest values were also from genes on A.ox_chr4_1 (median = 0.39) and A.ox_chr1_2 (median = 0.38) (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Fig7">7b</a>). However, statistical tests for comparison between A.ox_chr4_1 and other chromosomes/chromosomal segments were significant (Benjamini–Hochberg adjusted <i>P</i> &lt; 0.03, one-sided Mann–Whitney <i>U</i> test; Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S13</a>), while only two of the tests for A.ox_chr1_2 were significant (Benjamini–Hochberg adjusted <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, one-sided Mann–Whitney <i>U</i> test; Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S13</a>). The discrepancy between these two segments indicates that they may have evolved under different constraints.</p><p>Previous studies suggested that <i>Aquilegia</i> species are ancient tetraploids, but the timing of the whole-genome duplication (WGD) event is debated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Guo, L. et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science362, 343–347 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1623">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Akoz, G. &amp; Nordborg, M. The Aquilegia genome reveals a hybrid origin of core eudicots. Genome Biol.20, 256 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1626">31</a></sup>. Guo and colleagues (2018) held that this event occurred after the divergence of Papaveraceae and Ranunculaceae, while Akoz and Nordborg (2019) proposed that it was shared by all eudicots. To better understand this event, we identified all the within-genome syntenic regions of the <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> genome and calculated the synonymous substitution rate (<i>d</i><sub>S</sub>) of each pair of collinear paralogs using the modified Nei–Gojobori model. For comparison, the <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values of collinear paralogs in <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> and grape and those of the one-to-one orthologs between <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and grape were also estimated. The distributions of <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values for collinear paralogs in the two <i>Aquilegia</i> species peaked at the same position (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S7</a>), confirming that <i>Aquilegia</i> species are ancient tetraploids. Although the grape paralogs had a similar peak value on the distribution plot of <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values, the <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> distribution of orthologs of <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and grape peaked at a higher value, indicating that the WGD event occurred after the divergence of <i>Aquilegia</i> and grape. Therefore, our results strongly support that the most recent WGD in <i>Aquilegia</i> occurred after the divergence of Papaveraceae and Ranunculaceae, which is not consistent with the previously hypothesized pre-eudicot tetraploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Guo, L. et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science362, 343–347 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1702">30</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">A high-quality genome of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>
</h3><p>In this study, by combining PacBio, BioNano, and Hi–C data, we have built a nearly complete assembly of the genome of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>. The evaluation results show that the genome is of high quality. In comparison with the <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> genome v3.1, the assembly of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var<i>. kansuensis</i> has better contiguity, with longer contig N50, fewer contigs/scaffolds, and a lower percentage of gaps (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#Tab1">1</a>). Notably, the 7 chromosomes of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> were supported by superscaffolds almost as long as chromosome arms. The biggest difference between these two genomes is the total number of protein-coding genes; the <i>A. coerulea</i> genome is annotated with 4452 more genes. Our detailed comparison revealed that a great part of this discrepancy can be attributed to the many more species-specific genes in <i>A. coerulea</i>. Two underlying reasons might account for this. First, these genes were generated de novo or were lost in other species, which may have played important roles in interspecific morphological or physiological divergence. Second, since gene predictions are not error-free, it is possible that some of them have been generated by misannotation. The observation that the mRNA length of the species-specific group was significantly shorter than the genome-wide average (<i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−199</sup> and <i>P</i> = 0 for <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>, respectively) provides further support for this possibility. Nevertheless, 36.2% (815/2250) of the species-specific genes in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> have Gene Ontology (GO) assignments, compared with only 18.6% (951/5119) in <i>A. coerulea</i>, suggesting that our gene set is of higher confidence. In fact, our annotation is supported by much stronger evidence since we applied both Iso-Seq and RNA-Seq (PE150) data from different tissue samples (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S8</a>) to annotate the <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> genome, in contrast to the annotation of <i>A. coerulea</i>, for which only RNA-Seq (SE35 and SE40) from sepals of this species but numerous sequencing data (mostly SE51) from other species were used. The fact that 97.2% of the genes in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> show homology with functionally annotated genes reaffirms the high confidence of our gene set. All these results show that we obtained a high-quality genome that will pave the way for spur reduction, adaptation, and evolutionary studies of <i>Aquilegia</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genome divergence between <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and <i>A. coerulea</i>
</h3><p>It is interesting that the two <i>Aquilegia</i> species show considerable differences in their genomes. First, there has been a translocation between chromosomes 1 (A.ox_chr1) and 4 (A.ox_chr4) in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> relative to <i>A. coerulea</i>. Previously, it was observed that Chr4 in two other <i>Aquilegia</i> and one <i>Semiaquilegia</i> species have similar organizational patterns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1847">10</a></sup>, suggesting that this chromosome might have a conserved structure. One important piece of evidence is that 5S rDNA loci are uniquely localized to Chr4 in all three species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1851">10</a></sup>. We found that in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>, 5S rDNA loci are located on both A.ox_chr1 and A.ox_chr4, the majority of which are on the segments that are homologous to Chr4 of <i>A. coerulea</i> (Aco_chr4) (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S14</a>). Thus, it is likely that the translocation event was specific to <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>. More interestingly, we also found 5S rDNA loci on other segments, including the 2,542,608–2,542,670 and 15,215,141–15,215,260 regions of A.ox_chr1 and the 33,260,556–33,260,618 region of A.ox_chr4 (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S14</a>). This indicates the possibility that the 5S rDNA was not unique to Aox_chr4 even before the chromosome translocation. Second, the two genomes are different in the number and content of annotated protein-coding genes, transposable elements, and noncoding RNAs. Particularly, among protein-coding genes, only 68.0% (17,391/25,571) of the genes of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> are one-to-one orthologous to genes of <i>A. coerulea</i>, which means that numerous orthologs are different in copy number. Third, it has been reported that the sequence divergence between species of <i>Aquilegia</i> from different geographic regions (such as Asia and North America) is at least 0.81%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1890">10</a></sup>. Since a divergence level as low as 0.15% would lead to marked biases in some genetic/genomic analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Nevado, B., Ramos-Onsins, S. E. &amp; Perez‐Enciso, M. Resequencing studies of nonmodel organisms using closely related reference genomes: optimal experimental designs and bioinformatics approaches for population genomics. Mol. Ecol.23, 1764–1779 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1894">32</a></sup>, using either one of the genomes as the reference for the other would be unsatisfactory. These results demonstrate the evolution of chromosomes in the plant genome and further highlight the importance of sequencing the genome of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Special evolutionary pattern of Chr4 of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>
</h3><p>It has been shown that the entire Chr4 of <i>A. coerulea</i> (A.co_chr4) has evolved uniquely under reduced purifying selection, demonstrating a “decaying” nature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Akoz, G. &amp; Nordborg, M. The Aquilegia genome reveals a hybrid origin of core eudicots. Genome Biol.20, 256 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1922">31</a></sup> with a much higher level of polymorphism and lower gene density than other chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1926">10</a></sup>. Moreover, it is suggested that this evolutionary pattern of Chr4 began before the split of <i>Aquilegia</i> and <i>Semiaquilegia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Filiault, D. L. et al. The Aquilegia genome provides insight into adaptive radiation and reveals an extraordinarily polymorphic chromosome with a unique history. Elife7, e36426 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27764e1935">10</a></sup>. In concordance with this, we found that the chromosome segments in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> (A.ox_chr4_1 and A.ox_chr1_2) that are homologous to A.co_chr4 have a significantly lower gene density. What is surprising is that A.ox_chr4_1 and A.ox_chr1_2 do not show the same pattern in their <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> ratios. A.ox_chr4_1 has a significantly higher <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> ratio than other chromosomes or chromosome segments, while the difference for A.ox_chr1_2 is weaker. This indicates that the translocation of this segment to Chr1 might have influenced the evolution of genes therein so that they are under more functional constraints than before. In this case, it is tempting to speculate that the translocation of a segment (A.ox_chr4_2) from Chr1, which is likely evolving under high functional constraint (high gene density and low <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> ratio), might also have an impact on the “decay” of Chr4. More genomic data from other <i>Aquilegia</i> taxa would be helpful to further clarify this influence.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant material collection, DNA extraction, and sequencing</h3><p>We excavated whole plants of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var<i>. kansuensis</i> from a population in Yuzhong County, Gansu Province, China (N35°47′33″, E104°3′12″), and cultivated them in a growth chamber. Fresh young leaves of an individual were collected, and genomic DNA was extracted using the TIANGEN DNAsecure Plant Kit (GP1). For short-read sequencing, an ~350 bp insert size pair-end library was constructed and sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 4000 platform. A total of 18.3 Gb raw data were generated (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S1</a>). For long-read sequencing, ~20 kb SMRTbell libraries were prepared and sequenced using PacBio Sequel Sequencer, which produced 37.6 Gb raw data (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><p>To extract enough DNA for the construction of optical mapping libraries, we collected young, fresh leaves from dark-treated seedlings of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>. High-molecular-weight DNAs were then isolated using the BioNano Prep Plant Tissue DNA Isolation Kit Contents (Part # 80003). Direct Labeling Enzyme 1 (DLE-1) was used to digest the DNAs, which were then fluorescently labeled, stained, and loaded onto a Saphyr Chip for sequencing. Nearly 301 Gb optical mapping data were generated.
