For the Hi–C data, we constructed two libraries, which were subjected to sequencing on the Illumina HiSeq 4000 platform, yielding a total of 36.3 Gb data (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>PacBio SMRT long reads were assembled using Falcon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2020">33</a></sup> (Branch 3.1) (--max_diff 100 --max_cov 100 --min_cov 2 --min_len 5000) after self-correction. The resulting contigs were then polished by Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2024">34</a></sup> using the long reads. SSPACE-LongRead<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinform.15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2028">35</a></sup> was applied to merge the contigs into scaffolds with default parameters. Finally, Illumina reads were mapped back to polish the scaffolds using Pilon (—threads 20 —frags)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2032">36</a></sup>.</p><p>Genome map assemblies for <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> were generated using Bionano Solve Pipeline version 3.3 and Bionano Access version 1.3.0 (<a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/">https://bionanogenomics.com/support/software-downloads/</a>). Low-quality optical molecules with length ≤ 180 kb or label number ≤9 were removed. A rough assembly was first performed with the following parameters: -i 0 -V 0 -A -z -u –m. A second assembly, using the first round result as reference, was launched with the following parameters: -y -r (rough assembly cmap) -V 0 -m. To create hybrid scaffolds, optical maps were aligned to PacBio-assembled contigs and scaffolded with BioNano’s hybrid-scaffold tool. The process included comparing the BioNano genome nick-based maps to the in silico nick maps of the genome sequence to find their best matches and potential reciprocal scaffolding of each dataset. If there were conflicts between the sequence and optical maps, both of them were cut at the conflict sites and assembled again with the hybrid-scaffold parameter “-B 2 -N 2”.</p><p>To construct chromosome-level assemblies, we further applied the 36.3 Gb Hi–C data. Almost 31.9 Gb clean data were retained after removing adapter sequences and low-quality reads, i.e., those with a ratio of N higher than 0.1 and/or quality value less than 5 (<i>Q</i> &lt; 5). Unmapped reads, self-ligated reads, dangling-end reads, internal fragment reads and reads with incorrect sizes were further removed using HiCUP software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wingett, S. et al. HiCUP: pipeline for mapping and processing Hi-C data. F1000Res4, 1310 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2055">37</a></sup>. Hi–C reads were mapped to the hybrid scaffolds, and Hi–C contact frequency between genomic loci was computed using Juicer (version 1.7.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst.3, 95–98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2059">26</a></sup>. 3D-DNA (version 180114)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2063">21</a></sup> was used to anchor and orient scaffolds based on the contact frequency calculated from mapped Hi–C read pairs to obtain the pseudomolecules for two rounds with default parameters. During this process, we manually corrected the misassembled order, oriented scaffolds of DNA based on Hi–C data, and took advantage of the telomere-to-telomere contact enrichment associated with genomes in the Rabl configuration to obtain seven pseudochromosomes using Juicebox Assembly Tools (JBAT version 1.8.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. The Juicebox Assembly Tools module facilitates de novo assembly of mammalian genomes with chromosome-length scaffolds for under $1000. Preprint at bioRxiv

https://doi.org/10.1101/254797

 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2067">38</a></sup>. The Hi–C read contact frequency matrix was visualized using Juicebox (version 1.8.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. The Juicebox Assembly Tools module facilitates de novo assembly of mammalian genomes with chromosome-length scaffolds for under $1000. Preprint at bioRxiv

https://doi.org/10.1101/254797

 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2072">38</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Genome annotation</h3><p>Two methods (i.e., homology alignment and de novo annotation) were used to extract the repeats in the genome of <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>. The repeats in a plant genome can be divided into tandem repeats and interspersed repeats. For tandem repeats, the software TRF version 4.09 (<a href="http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html">http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html</a>) was used to make de novo predictions. For the prediction of transposable elements, two approaches were used: the first was to search against the Repbase database (<a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase">http://www.girinst.org/repbase</a>) using RepeatMasker (v4.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Tarailo‐Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform.Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2098">39</a></sup> with default parameters, and the second was de novo prediction through LTR_FINDER (v1.0.7) (<a href="http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/">http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/</a>), RepeatScout (v1.0.5) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>), and RepeatModeler (v1.0.3) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) with default parameters.</p><p>Noncoding RNAs (ncRNAs) include tRNA, rRNA, miRNA, and snRNA. tRNA was predicted by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res.25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2118">40</a></sup>. rRNA was annotated by BLASTN searches against other species. miRNAs and snRNAs were identified by searching against the Rfam database (13.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kalvari, I. et al. Rfam 13.0: shifting to a genome-centric resource for non-coding RNA families. Nucleic Acids Res.46, D335–D342 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2122">41</a></sup> with default parameters using INFERNAL software (v1.1.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics29, 2933–2935 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2126">42</a></sup>.</p><p>A combination of three methods, including ab initio prediction, homology-based prediction and transcriptome-assisted prediction, was used to identify protein-coding genes. For homology-based prediction, we used sequences of six species, including <i>A. coerulea</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>A. trichopoda</i>, and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> from Phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/</a>); <i>A. thaliana</i> from TAIR (<a href="https://www.arabidopsis.org">https://www.arabidopsis.org</a>); and <i>P. somniferum</i> from communications with the relevant authors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Guo, L. et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science362, 343–347 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2160">30</a></sup>. TBLASTN searches (<i>e</i>-value ≤ 1e−5) were then conducted against the <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> assembly to identify homologous proteins, which were then aligned to the assembly by GeneWise (v2.4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res.14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2174">43</a></sup> to annotate gene structures. For ab initio prediction, we employed Augustus (v3.2.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res.34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2178">44</a></sup>, Geneid (v1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Alioto, T., Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; Guigó, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform.64, e56 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2182">45</a></sup>, Genescan (v1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol.268, 78–94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2186">46</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (v3.04)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2190">47</a></sup>, and SNAP (v2013.11.29)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinform.5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2195">48</a></sup> software with default parameters. We used both Iso-Seq and RNA-Seq datasets from different tissue samples (i.e., roots, stems, leaves, flowers, fruits, and seeds) at different stages of development as evidence for gene annotation. We constructed five Iso-Seq libraries, which contained RNAs isolated from five samples, respectively, i.e., leaves of different stages, stems+roots, seedlings, small inflorescences (with flowers of early stages), and large inflorescences (with flowers of medium-late stages and seeds). These libraries were then sequenced on the PacBio Sequel platform, yielding a total of 47.6 Gb raw data. RNA-Seq data were generated by pooling equal amounts of RNA obtained from the above five samples and sequencing on the Illumina HiSeq 4000 platform. RNA-Seq raw reads were filtered and mapped to our genome assembly using TopHat (v2.0.11)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2199">49</a></sup> to identify exon regions and splice positions. The alignment results were then input into Cufflinks (v2.2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc.7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2203">50</a></sup> with default parameters for genome-based transcript assembly. Iso-Seq data were processed through the standard Iso-Seq pipeline. The generated transcripts, together with the genome-guided assembly of RNA-Seq data, were integrated with the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments (PASA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol.9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2207">51</a></sup>. A nonredundant reference gene set was generated by merging genes predicted by the three aforementioned methods with EVidenceModeler (EVM, v1.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol.9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2211">51</a></sup> and then updated using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol.9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2215">51</a></sup>. Functions were assigned to each gene according to its best matches by aligning its protein sequence to the Swiss-Prot and NR databases using BLASTP (<i>e</i>-value ≤ 1e−5). Motifs and domains were annotated using InterProScan70 (v5.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2223">52</a></sup> by searching against publicly available databases, including ProDom, PRINTS, Pfam, SMRT, PANTHER, and PROSITE. The GO terms for each gene were assigned according to the corresponding InterPro entry.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Genome alignment and microsynteny detection</h3><p>We used Nucmer, contained in the MUMmer package version 3.23<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Marcais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol.14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2235">53</a></sup>, to align the genomes of the two species, with default parameters. The results were then filtered by delta-filter program with “-i 90 -g –q”. To infer gene-level synteny, we used BLASTP (-evalue 1e−20 -num_threads 16 -outfmt 6) to generate protein alignment between the two species. The outputs were then imported into MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res.40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2239">54</a></sup> to identify syntenic blocks.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Orthogroup and gene family classification</h3><p>OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol.20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2252">27</a></sup> (orthofinder -f fastadata -S blast -M msa -T raxml) software was used to group genes of different species, and in-house R scripts were applied to count the number of ortholog clusters obtained. Based on gene family information of <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i> (<a href="http://www.supfam.org/genome/Ac">http://www.supfam.org/genome/Ac</a>), we performed BLASTP (-gapopen 11 -gapextend 1 -max_target_seqs 5) searches of each <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> sequence against the proteome of <i>A. coerulea</i> and assigned it to a certain gene family based on the best hit.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Phylogenetic analysis of the MADS-box gene family</h3><p>We first retrieved all MADS-box gene sequences classified by using the gene family information of <i>A</i>. <i>coerulea</i>. When aligning the protein sequences of these genes, we found five in <i>A. coerulea</i> and seven in <i>A. oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> that had incomplete MADS-box domains. We mapped these sequences to their corresponding genomic regions using TBLASTN and manually curated the annotations. All MADS-box protein sequences were aligned using the hmmalign program in the HMMER package version 3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Eddy, S. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2300">55</a></sup> and the SRF-domain (MADS-box domain) model downloaded from Pfam (<a href="http://pfam.sanger.ac.uk/">http://pfam.sanger.ac.uk/</a>). The corresponding CDS sequence alignment was generated using PAL2NAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res.34, W609–W612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2308">56</a></sup>. Nucleotide sites in the MADS-box domain were used for phylogenetic analysis, which was performed in MEGA7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA7: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 7.0 for bigger datasets. Mol. Biol. Evol.33, 1870–1874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2312">57</a></sup> (pairwise deletion and <i>p</i>-distance model) with 1000 bootstrap replications.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Gene density and <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> analyses</h3><p>Gene density was calculated for nonoverlapping 300 kb windows along the whole chromosome using in-house R scripts. Then the ggplot2 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wickham, H. ggplot2: Elegant Graphics for Data Analysis. (Springer, 2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2336">58</a></sup> in R version 3.6.2 was used for plotting, and the Wilcox test was employed for statistical analysis. One-to-one ortholog clusters of the two species identified using OrthoFinder were used for <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> analysis. Protein sequences of each pair of orthologs were aligned using MUSCLE3.8.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res.32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2348">59</a></sup> software with default parameters. PAL2NAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res.34, W609–W612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2353">56</a></sup> was then applied to create the corresponding CDS sequence alignment, and trimAL software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Capella-Gutiérrez, S., Silla-Martínez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldón, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2357">60</a></sup> was used to remove alignment-ambiguous codons. Finally, the Perl script (dS_dN_MNG.PL) downloaded from the website (<a href="https://sites.google.com/view/masafumi-nozawa/scripts">https://sites.google.com/view/masafumi-nozawa/scripts</a>) was applied to calculate the <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values using a modified Nei–Gojobori model, and in-house R scripts were used to calculate the <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> ratios.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Identification of collinear paralogs and calculation of <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values</h3><p>We used MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res.40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2395">54</a></sup> software to identify synteny blocks within each of the <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i>, <i>A. coerulea</i>, and grape genomes. One-to-one orthologs between <i>A</i>. <i>oxysepala</i> var. <i>kansuensis</i> and grape were obtained by using OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: phylogenetic orthology inference for comparative genomics. Genome Biol.20, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2421">27</a></sup>. The <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> value for each pair of genes was then calculated as described above using the modified Nei–Gojobori model. The geom_density<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA7: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 7.0 for bigger datasets. Mol. Biol. Evol.33, 1870–1874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0328-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d27764e2430">57</a></sup> function in R was used to compute and draw kernel density estimates of each <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> list.</p></div></div></section>

Y. Jiang, R.Z., J.X., and M.W. collected the samples. R.Z. and X.G. extracted the RNA. J.X. and K.L. assembled the genome and assessed genome quality with the help of Y. Jiao. M.W. and X.G. checked the RNA-seq data. J.X. carried out comparative genomics analysis with the assistance of H.Z. B.Y., J.X., and G.X. performed the phylogenetic analysis. H.Z. calculated the gene densities and <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> ratios, and conducted statistical analyses. H.Z. and G.X. provided R scripts for plotting. J.X., H.K., Y. Jiao, and G.X. wrote the paper. All authors read, edited, and approved the final paper.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
